## Excerpt

by Origen of Alexandria, written around 220-230 AD, is one of the most important and contentious works of early Christianity. It provoked controversy when written, provoked further debate when translated into Latin by Rufinus in the fourth century, and was the subject, together with its author,...

# Origen: On First Principles, Reader's Edition (Oxford Early Christian Texts)

 by [John Behr](https://www.amazon.com/John-Behr/e/B001HPIUAO/ref=dp_byline_cont_book_1)(Author)

[4.7 _4.7 out of 5 stars_](javascript:void(0))[(68)](https://www.amazon.com/Origen-First-Principles-Oxford-Christian/dp/0198845316#averageCustomerReviewsAnchor)

[Part of: Oxford Early Christian Texts (14 books)](https://www.amazon.com/dp/B0BXY6FK7G?binding=paperback&ref=dbs_dp_rwt_sb_pc_tpbk)

Sorry, there was a problem loading this page. Try again.

{"desktop_buybox_group_1":[{"displayPrice":"$51.49","priceAmount":51.49,"currencySymbol":"$","integerValue":"51","decimalSeparator":".","fractionalValue":"49","symbolPosition":"left","hasSpace":false,"showFractionalPartIfEmpty":true,"offerListingId":"oWao3%2FUnmtH8raXlfoyPfUJ1jGLNoBeBmZnRIqon4SVadzRJqO%2FUICwrUq0bmwMZQehsMfo4H%2F4t08wILm1lesntdZ9%2B%2F0lj8eeDvLWvAxB3jPEHQU7%2FE6kdKNpcFScPUr31G3RtE%2F6P6sGbEKn6fMGeG1KVhe9r1%2BDPH4874cn%2FTVNpyzFv0LEGKby7gcSQ","locale":"en-US","buyingOptionType":"NEW","aapiBuyingOptionIndex":0}]}

### Purchase options and add-ons

* * *

- [x] 

On First Principles by Origen of Alexandria, written around 220-230 AD, is one of the most important and contentious works of early Christianity. It provoked controversy when written, provoked further debate when translated into Latin by Rufinus in the fourth century, and was the subject, together with its author, of condemnation in the sixth century. As a result, the work no longer survives intact in the original Greek. We only have the complete work in the Latin translation of Rufinus, and a few extensive passages preserved in Greek by being excerpted into the Philokalia of Basil of Caesarea and Gregory of Nazianzus. 

John Behr offers a translation of one of the most important texts from early Christianity. He includes an invaluable introduction, which provides a clear structure of the work with significant implications for how the text is to be read and for understanding the character of theology in the early Christian tradition.

[Read more](javascript:void(0))

## Editorial Reviews

### Review

"Behr's translation is literal without being wooden or cumbersome. In keeping with the original languages, some sentences are long, but they are readable. Some detailed footnotes remain to guide readers." -- Christopher A. Stephenson, Religious Studies Review"Stirring and profound." -- M. J. Edwards, Journal of Ecclesiastical History"This translation should now be the point of departure for readers who seek to consult Origen's On First Principles in English ... This is a translation that can be used with students, and on which scholars uncertain in their Latin can rely. This is then a worthy replacement for that by Butterworth which has been the staple for generations of English-speaking readers ... In sum, this is an excellent translation, and a beautifully laid out text. Behr's vision of the work's structure is insightful." -- Lewis Ayres, International Journal of Systematic Theology

### About the Author

John Behr, Dean and Professor of Patristics, St Vladimir's Orthodox Theological Seminary, New York, and Metropolitan Kallistos Chair in Orthodox Theology, Vrije Universiteit, Amsterdam

John Behr is the Dean of St Vladimir's Orthodox Theological Seminary and Professor of Patristics and Metropolitan Kallistos Chair in Orthodox Theology at Vrije Universiteit. His previous publications include John the Theologian and his Paschal Gospel: A Prologue to Theology (2019), Asceticism and Anthropology in Irenaeus and Clement (2000), The Case Against Diodore and Theodore: Texts and Their Contexts (2011), and Irenaeus of Lyons: Identifying Christianity (2013). He is also the co-editor of The Role of Life in Death: A Multidisciplinary Examination of Issues pertaining to Life and Death (Wipf and Stock, 2015; with C. Cunningham).

[How customer reviews and ratings work](javascript:void(0))

Customer Reviews, including Product Star Ratings help customers to learn more about the product and decide whether it is the right product for them.

To calculate the overall star rating and percentage breakdown by star, we don’t use a simple average. Instead, our system considers things like how recent a review is and if the reviewer bought the item on Amazon. It also analyzed reviews to verify trustworthiness.

[Learn more how customers reviews work on Amazon](https://www.amazon.com/gp/help/customer/display.html/ref=cm_cr_dp_d_omni_lm_btn?nodeId=G8UYX7LALQC8V9KA)

_5 out of 5 stars_

##### A response to M.E.H’s review on the text’s type-set

I haven’t even started to read this book yet - it is MASSIVE - so this is not a review on the content or quality of the translation at all. This is a response to M.E.H.’s review, where he or she docked stars because of ostensibly poor quality control over the book’s typeset. Upon receiving and navigating to the section of the text where these “issues” begin, namely Book 3, it is apparent that Behr transitions into translating not a single manuscript, but two - Latin and Greek. There is actually nothing wrong with the type-set. From this point until then end of the text, the Latin translation is always on the top of each page (Latin footnotes included), and the Greek is on the bottom. There is an adequate amount of space inserted between the two to make this apparent as well. Perhaps it would’ve been helpful for Behr to clearly indicate this layout switch in his introduction - and maybe he did, but I don’t know because I haven’t read it (it’s quite a long and in depth intro). Nevertheless, I had to post this review with 5 stars to clarify an outright misunderstanding on the part of the other reviewer, and offset an undeserved low rating. The text itself is in fact perfectly fine. Blessings!

[More](javascript:void(0))[Hide](javascript:void(0))

[Read more](javascript:void(0))   ![Image 116: Customer image](https://images-na.ssl-images-amazon.com/images/G/01/x-locale/common/transparent-pixel._V192234675_.gif)  [![Image 117](https://m.media-amazon.com/images/S/amazon-avatars-global/default.png) DP](https://www.amazon.com/gp/profile/amzn1.account.AE2CQAUMN2DSZICKZEG6ZAMTT6WQ/ref=cm_cr_dp_d_gw_pop) _5.0 out of 5 stars_
##### A response to M.E.H’s review on the text’s type-set

 Reviewed in the United States on September 16, 2021   I haven’t even started to read this book yet - it is MASSIVE - so this is not a review on the content or quality of the translation at all. This is a response to M.E.H.’s review, where he or she docked stars because of ostensibly poor quality control over the book’s typeset.

Upon receiving and navigating to the section of the text where these “issues” begin, namely Book 3, it is apparent that Behr transitions into translating not a single manuscript, but two - Latin and Greek. There is actually nothing wrong with the type-set. From this point until then end of the text, the Latin translation is always on the top of each page (Latin footnotes included), and the Greek is on the bottom. There is an adequate amount of space inserted between the two to make this apparent as well.

Perhaps it would’ve been helpful for Behr to clearly indicate this layout switch in his introduction - and maybe he did, but I don’t know because I haven’t read it (it’s quite a long and in depth intro). Nevertheless, I had to post this review with 5 stars to clarify an outright misunderstanding on the part of the other reviewer, and offset an undeserved low rating. The text itself is in fact perfectly fine.

Blessings!

*   [![Image 121](https://images-na.ssl-images-amazon.com/images/G/01/x-locale/common/grey-pixel.gif) Hope Helpful](https://www.amazon.com/gp/profile/amzn1.account.AEHLQTHTOVETCRQ3P7EDDIRCMCEQ) ##### [_5.0 out of 5 stars_ Clear, fascinating introduction](https://www.amazon.com/gp/customer-reviews/R1BS67L84A2G1H/ref=cm_cr_dp_d_rvw_ttl?ie=UTF8) Reviewed in the United States on January 15, 2023 Format: Paperback[Verified Purchase](https://www.amazon.com/gp/help/customer/display.html/ref=cm_cr_dp_d_rvw_avp?nodeId=G8UYX7LALQC8V9KA) Origen was Christianity's first theologian. There was no template for what Christian theology could/would/should be so Origen used analytic approaches of Greek philosophers to try to extract principles embedded in Scripture. Some of his conclusions were thrown out (burned/totally destroyed) by later theologians, though Origen continued to be regarded as a saint.

I found the unusually long introduction by the translator to be fascinating and very helpful.

In his attempt to present the entire corpus, the translator has also included references to some of Origen's lost (destroyed) texts that were quoted or summarized by other early authors. The first two books were good to read through, but starting in the third book I noticed that the offsets between the Latin and Greek translations are offset in such a manner that one will start midway between the prior translation and the prior's end. What I mean is that 3.1.2 Latin has two sentences on pate 147 before the footnote that ties to 3.1.1 of the Latin translation is presented. Then, 3.1.2 of the Latin is interrupted by 3.1.1 of the Greek on the same page, before 3.1.2 of the Latin picks up again on page 148. This is continued by 3.1.2 of the Greek on page 148 being interrupted by 3.1.3 of the Latin at the top of page 149 only to continue mid-page 149.

I'm very disappointed that this would make it out of the printer with such poor type-set quality.
